B00004TEUX|5.0|This CD features what is almost certainly the greatest recorded performance of Mendelssohn's best symphony, the Symphony No. 3, \\"Scottish.\\" Although eclipsed in popularity by the \\"Italian Symphony,\\" the Scottish Symphony is the most mature symphonic statement by Mendelssohn and the last symphony he completed, in 1842 (despite being no. 3 of 5!). Peter Maag's 1960 performance with the London Symphony Orchestra has long been regarded as the \\"reference\\" performance of this work: Maag simply got everything right here, from tempos (always spot-on), clarity of textures, the right atmosphere and an overriding sense of excitement and discovery at every turn. Pay special attention to how Maag handles the coda at the finale of the symphony, with the horns blaring out with such power and clarity. I have yet to hear a performance which surpasses Maag's, including his own digital remakes with a Spanish orchestra. The sound quality is absolutely great for its time and in this 24/96 remastering it sounds better than ever before.    The second half of the disc contains what many regard as Mendelssohn's greatest work: his Overture and Incidental Music to A Midsummer Night's Dream. The Overture, written when Mendelssohn was a teenager is a delightful and joyous work and it is admirably complemented by the music he later wrote to accompany Shakespeare's play. The tracks featured here include all the best numbers, leaving out only the repetitious movements. Each and every moment of this music features something we do not normally associate with the German repertoire: color. Maag ensures that the maximum amount of color is drawn out of each movement, which makes his performance eclipse even Szell's virtuosic but dryer Cleveland performance of many of these movements on Sony. Although recorded three years earlier than the Scottish Symphony, Maag's Midsummer still features first-rate sound, again given excellent remastering here.    In my opinion, this CD contains Mendelssohn's two greatest compositions: his best symphony and one of the most beloved of all \\"incidental music\\" ever composed (his Violin Concerto is a very close third favorite of all Mendelssohn compositions). The performances of these two works featured here are also the greatest performances of these works ever committed to disc (at least to my ears). At slightly under 80 minutes, this CD is also very generous. Don't hesitate to grab this CD before it goes out of print!
B0032UYFA6|5.0|As much as I like this show, it's the sort of drama that throws into sharp relief the difficulties in telling plausible character-driven (as opposed to plot-driven) stories. The writers have the sense to avoid \\"on the nose\\" dialog that tells us what the characters are thinking (especially their motivations), but this leads to occasional confusion as to What, Exactly, Is Going On, And Why? There is also some out-of-character dialog, such as Jesse making profound and subtle remarks, and Hank not being his usual doofus self, for no good reason.<br /><br />Skyler remains the principal villain of the piece. She finally starts supporting Walter -- but principally for financial reasons.<br /><br />The logical and scientific errors continue. 0345529057|5.0|This is purely a review of the work itself and not the packaging. I personally recommend getting all four at once and going at it. (Buy the fifth too!) To my mind, Martin is the first author in his genre to exceed the innovations and uniqueness of Tolkien's Lord of the Rings series, bringing the fantasy genre completely up to date, extending the milieu, characterization, cultural complexity and presentation far beyond his rightly esteemed predecessor. Spoilers ahead: essentially, where Tolkien is black-and-white, Martin deals in shades of grey. His books are sexually explicit. The High Middle Ages milieu is carefully rendered, complicated, violent bordering on anarchy, and almost pitilessly realistic. He shows no compunction in killing off main or major characters. (This is one of the things I like most about him.) Magic and magical creatures are sparsely deployed, especially in the first volume, and when appearing can be rather repellent in their actuality. There are no Gandalf-figures in this series. There is none of the Dungeons & Dragons atmosphere so prevalent in popular fantasy genre books. By the middle of the fourth book, the main continent of the series, Westeros, is pretty much a wreck and most of the major characters are dead or missing. (This is a major letdown for many readers.)<br /><br />Book 1 - A Game of Thrones: sets up many threads and trends for the later books, introduces many characters, some of which make it to the fourth book. Some characters and medieval powers seem to be set up as the Forces For Good, and others as the Forces For Bad. The entire book is gripping and fascinating, and gives serious indications of the unpredictableness to come.<br /><br />Book 2 - A Clash of Kings: The series takes flight in this book. There is no other way for me to put it - you simply will not be able to put this book down. You will bounce around between a dozen or so main characters and meet so many other major figures peopling each section, and yet the sense of constant movement and engagement with all characters is unceasing. Great suspense is built as the monsters beyond the Wall continue to threaten, and the Black Brothers resolve to confront the threat. A unifying king dies and armies march.<br /><br />Book 3 - Storm of Swords: Ditto with the third book. Un-put-down-able. Climactic battles take place at the Wall, and in Westeros, and in the keep at King's Landing. Here it is completely clear that the \\"side\\" previously considered the 'good guys' - the Wolf forces superficially headed by the Stark clan - are just as bloody-minded and warlike as the Lion forces headed by the Lannisters. Some very ugly events take place in this book, but they are never less than utterly gripping for the reader. By the time this book ends, you will be on a trembling edge of eagerness for the fourth book and What Comes Next.<br /><br />Book 4 - Feast For Crows: Widely considered a letdown, and to some extent it is, since the only really sympathetic character turns out to be Jaime Lannister, other than Samwell, who is almost unmistakably the authorial alter ego. The continent, as I noted, is pretty much wrecked except for the southern reaches of Highgarden and Dorne. An island nation suddenly becomes far more aggressive. The dwarf disappears. The dragon queen makes no appearance. The character of Brienne takes up far too much space in this book, only to meet a very ugly end. Poor Arya hides out in Braavos. Nevertheless, this book takes off again in the last 300 pages or so and ends in an uptempo fashion, with Jaime cleverly resolving a siege and another depth-charge detonation of a major character, also with a very clever change in fortunes for the tragic Sansa. Setting things up very well for the fifth book, forthcoming a few weeks after this review was written!<br /><br />Tolkien is great and I love him and his books. Nevertheless, it was time for someone to come along and surpass him. I think Martin innovates in this genre to such anextent that even when he takes risks that don't work, as much of Book 4 does, it's still worth reading and and when it does work, as it does pretty much all the time otherwise, the series transcends genre literature. He breaks out of the D&D concept completely and makes a fascinating real-world concept out of something that seemed impossible. These books will be remembered for a long time.that don't work, as much of Book 4 does, it's still worth reading and and when it does work, as it does pretty much all the time otherwise, the series transcends genre literature. He breaks out of the D&D concept completely and makes a fascinating real-world concept out of something that seemed impossible. These books will be remembered for a long time.

B0000CBHOI|5.0|It comes down to what you might be looking for. If you want a concert, if you want a documentary of one of the particular \\"blues gods,\\" this might be too overwhelming for you. It has numerous discs, yes. It is quite long, yes.  But that is because it's a chronology of this music. It follows not only the music itself but the foundations of it: The social history. It shows what the music is about. If you are looking to see B.B. King play his famous style of guitar, it's here. But there are scenes that discuss what his life was like before he ever played. It discusses social unrest, poverty, even slavery. One of the best discs here involves a narrator taking a trip to western Africa, studying the music there and how it relates to what we now call the blues, talking to and playing with African musicians who don't even speak English. Later discs show what Robert Johnson's life was like before he made his classic recordings in the 1930's. You get to see all the other main men, like Buddy Guy, Willie Dixon, Muddy Waters, all of them. You get to see actual interviews with most of them.  And, later, you see Eric Clapton, Jeff Beck, and others talking about what they felt like to first hear this music form, how and why they started, what kept them dedicated to learning it.<br /><br />And the music? Is there live, loud, clear sounding blues? YES! You bet your you-know-what there is. It is here, and it is hot. It's just not all concentrated at once. You don't get to see all the great blues guys on the same stage during the entire thing. If that is what you're looking for, you might want to get one of the Eric Clapton \\"Crossroads\\" videos. They are great, and he still makes them every year with the latest players. This goes deeper... This shows you not only the music itself but the social fabric that created it and allowed it to grow through all those past generations. ENJOY!

0316098329|3.0|\\"When all is said the greatest action is to limit and isolate one's self.\\"  -- Johann Wolfgang Von Goethe\\"    \\"The smaller the world, the more complete the knowledge of the writer, therefore the greater his creative choices.\\"  --- Robert McKee, Story    \\"Now I'm in Outside but it turns out lots of it isn't real at all.\\"  --- Jack from Room    I really wanted to like this book.  The premise, the ambitious POV, it was all very enticing.  But, alas, as is so often the case - it doesn't quite deliver on its promise.    Plot-wise it is pretty standard fare, following the journey of Prisoner, Escape, Real World, Adjustment Pains, Epiphany, etc. (you even get the classic insensitive foster mom!)  It may have been more interesting if they'd failed to escape and instead fell into a psychosis, the narrative becoming more and more surreal.  In that sense - being severely limited gives you limitless imagination.      Another unexplored dynamic and something that could've really catapulted this story to the ranks of Timeless is: How does a Mother who's been raped deal with seeing the features of the rapist on her child? Is not the child, in a psychological sense, also a rapist?  Wouldn't the Mother start to unravel or at least be conflicted, especially since she's a prisoner?  To have witnessed this from the child's POV would've been truly something.  Too bad it's left on the back burner.    The first 100 pages are what Jack and his Mom do in the Room to occupy the time.  They play the same games over and over, read the same books over and over and cook and eat and look at skylight and cook and eat and really I don't think you need 100 pages of that.  Half would've more than sufficed to get the monotony of their routine.      Then there's Jack's inability to understand figures of speech, e.g. -    The Mom: \\"I think I'm losing my mind.\\"  Jack: \\"It's right here.\\"  I tap on her hair.      The Mom: \\"If he's the least bit human...\\"  Jack: Then what are his other bits?    And so on.It's cute, but gets old (and annoying) pretty quickly.   Again, less would've been better.  Though occasionally the boy does say some trenchant things, like the quote above and this bit on p. 287 (which is my favorite):    \\"I guess the time gets spread very thin like butter over all the world, the roads and houses and playgrounds and stores, so there's only a little smear of time on each place, then everyone has to hurry on to the next bit.\\"    I also thought \\"scave\\" was a cool invention, a blending of scared and brave.  \\"Scare is what you're feeling.  But brave is what you're doing.\\"    But these are the highlights.  Most of the book feels repetitive and predictable.  And the escape plan was totally crazy (see my \\"Spoiler Alert\\" at the bottom).      This is one of those books that could've been a true classic but falls short because it opts for plot convenience and narrative ease.  Donoghue could've limited herself even more - give Jack and Mom no TV, no skylight, no access to the sun (as was the case with the real-life Fritz children) and see how the boy would've made sense of the Outside then.  Would've been more interesting, much more of a shock, surreal, etc.  Again, a lot of potential here that wasn't fully mined.    I give it three stars for its attempt at a unique POV.    ***SPOILER ALERT***  I found the mom's plan for escape to be unbelievably far-fetched, certain to fail.   There were so many things that could've (and should've) gone wrong.  First off, the Outside would be so disorientating to Jack he would've very likely gotten confused and unable to carry out the plan.  Second, Old Nick would've treated him roughly, hauling him around, dumping the rug he was in onto cement or whatnot.   I could go on.  But once you read it I think you'll know what I mean.  Again, another too convenient device.

B004VFYPTW|5.0|Finally one of the most requested recordings in hard rock gets an official release in June 2011 by \\"Frontiers Records\\". This is arguably \\"Whitesnake\\" at the peak of their international popularity headlining the \\"Monsters of rock\\" festival in the UK with the legendary line up of David Coverdale (v), Andrian Vandenberg (g), Steve Vai (g), Rudy Sarzo (b) and Tommy Aldridge (d).<br /><br />BACKGROUND OF WHITESNAKE AT DONNINGTON<br /><br />This 2CD plus DVD set is a perfect documentation of \\"Whitesnake\\"s headline performance at the \\"Monster of Rock 1990\\" festival at Donnington, England. \\"Whitesnake\\" had previously performed at \\"Monsters of Rock\\" in 1980 and 1983-at which time they also headlined and shot a live video for a domestic release entitled \\"Whitesnake Commandos\\".<br /><br />THE RECORDINGS AND SURROUNDING CIRCUMSTANCES<br /><br />It has to be addressed that the recordings of audio and video were NEVER intended to serve for a commercial release. The audio was recorded and transmitted live by the BBC radio; the video served as real-time footage for the large video-walls of the event so that all 75.000 people could have a decent view of what was happening on stage.<br />The sound has been remixed and re-mastered to perfection, all instruments come through clearly, capturing each musician's distinct sound. Tommy Adridge's drum attack is up front in the mix with all toms and cymbals audible, Rudy Sarzo's bass is clean and ever-present while the guitars are perfectly defined bringing every minor improvisation to front, you will actually be able to tell the difference between Vandenberg's and Vai's sound from the CD alone.<br /><br />The video, as mentioned above, was professionally shot but not for the purpose of a video-release, simply for direct transmission. This<br />practically implies that you get many cameras covering the event but not all angles are perfect, in addition the definition is found to be slightly lacking and finally the colours are rather imbalanced. At the end of the day, however, the content more than makes up for the lack of HD: you get all the rock-star poses from the band, all stage theatrics have been captured and most importantly all members have a respectable video presence on each and every song, so you get to see all five snakes playing and moving around the large stage.<br /><br />EXTRA FEATURES<br /><br />The DVD provides the option for 2.0 stereo and 5.1 surround sound which is quite a feat for an early recording as this; both options are impeccable. In terms of bonus material there is a gallery with stills from the same era but most importantly an extensive behind the scenes video. This video goes through the recording process of the multi platinum \\"Slip of the tongue\\" album finding the band in the studio near lake Tahoe testing ideas and having fun, the entry of Steve Vai in the line up, the process of filming the promo videos for the album as well as live footage, all topped by commentary by David Cloverdale who is the ideal narrator for such a journey.<br /><br />OVERALL<br /><br />\\"Live at Donnington 1990\\" is a breathtaking documentation of the \\"Whitesnake\\" golden era and captures nothing less than a band at the top of hard rock/hair metal world. Whatever limitations of the footage do not make this package any less enjoyable and certainly take nothing away from the amazing performances that were delivered that night. Needless to say, this is a must for anyone in 80s rock.content more than makes up for the lack of HD: you get all the rock-star poses from the band, all stage theatrics have been captured and most importantly all members have a respectable video presence on each and every song, so you get to see all five snakes playing and moving around the large stage. B0036XN2L2|4.0|After an extremely long, influential, and storied career complete that spanned eighteen (!) studio efforts, four live releases,  twenty-three compilations/\\"best-of's,\\" several (i.e. at least five) side-projects, a truckload of famous hit singles, a ton of touring, and eighty million records sold worldwide, hard rock/heavy metal/glam primitives Scorpions have decided to call it a career. But fear not, fans, because this dissolution came not before dropping another new full-length. \\"Sting Of The Tail,\\" was released in 2010, marking their forty-fifth anniversary of being a band. Groups that are anywhere near as old as this are exceedingly rare to come by, anymore; and many of those that are still around seem to have lost their touch long ago. But The Scorpions put to rest any remaining doubt that you might have had once and f or all. On \\"Sting,\\" they once again offer more than enough proof that they are still extremely energized, inspired, potent, and well-oiled. And they might verge on sounding a little bit dated at times, here, but overall, they can still rock mighty hard. In fact, one could make the argument that they can still rock the house with the most of the best of `em. `<br /><br />These eleven new songs can be broken down into four parts. Numbers one, \\"Raised On Rock,\\" two (which is vintage glam), the pure speed metal of number six, \\"Rock Zone,\\" and eight, \\"Turn You On,\\" are all surprisingly aggressive and up-tempo. They are bolstered by fiery, chug and churn riffing, strong hooks, memorable choruses, power metal harmonies, occasional ripping guitar solos, and prominent, muscular, underlying bass lines. Also make sure you pay special attention to the last of these tunes, because it \\"rocks like heaven and hell\\"; and features a climactic and epic section with the record's best soloing. Next, there are big riff rockers like \\"Slave Me,\\" (which is very reminiscent of Dokken and Dio, and sports fist-pumping vocals and careening shred solos), \\"No Limit,\\" and \\"Spirit Of Rock.\\" Finally, there is a fair amount of material that is very lush, Eighties-esque power balladry. \\"The Good Die Young\\" is but one example, and it is long on soaring melodies, clean, pensive singing, docile harmonies, crunchy  choruses, and screaming guitar solos. \\"Lorelei,\\" with its warm and pretty acoustic strums, the very Iron Maiden/Queensryche-inspired \\"Sly,\\", and the bloated, and vocal hook-saturated closer, the ironically-entitled \\"The Best Is Yet To Come\\", follow-suit later on.<br /><br />It might not be the best Scorpions album of all time, but it is regardless a pretty darn good and rock solid one. It might not break down any walls, but it is most important for it to be an enjoyable listen. And that it is - very enjoyable. (All fans should be pleased with the \\"sting\\" left by the band this time around.) And one other thing is for certain: If this is the last we will ever hear from the Scorpions, then at least they went out sounding as poisonous as ever.k.\\" Finally, there is a fair amount of material that is very lush, Eighties-esque power balladry. \\"The Good Die Young\\" is but one example, and it is long on soaring melodies, clean, pensive singing, docile harmonies, crunchy  choruses, and screaming guitar solos. \\"Lorelei,\\" with its warm and pretty acoustic strums, the very Iron Maiden/Queensryche-inspired \\"Sly,\\", and the bloated, and vocal hook-saturated closer, the ironically-entitled \\"The Best Is Yet To Come\\", follow-suit later on. B0034G4ORC|4.0|At it's heart, Battle: Los Angeles is yet another retelling of H. G. Wells' seminal story, War of the Worlds. Like that classic invasion story, this one begins with a meteor shower that takes a turn for the worse when it becomes apparent that those meteors are actually extraterrestrial vessels containing a deadly cargo of alien soldiers hellbent on conquering our world for our natural resources, especially our water.    What unfolds is a world war where the unprepared forces of Earth must confront an unexpected threat from beyond. Thrust into this maelstrom is a Marine unit, largely lead by Sgt. Michael Nantz, tasked with rescuing some civilians who are trapped in a police station that is now behind enemy lines. As you can probably figure out on your own, when you send a Marine unit on a collision course with alien invaders, well, your gonna get a lot of fireworks.    So, that's the summation. Does this movie work?    I don't know what movie the critics were watching, but it wasn't the awesomely entertaining Battle: Los Angeles I watched last night!    Now, don't get me wrong - this isn't a perfect movie, nor a completely original one - but nonetheless I found BLA (I'm tired of writing out the name ) to be a throwback to classic sci-fi film making of the 1950's - you know the type: those classic alien invasion tales heavily influenced by the Red Scare; the \\"aliens are evil and need to be gun-downed by red blooded Americans\\" type of tale that they haven't made in a long while. On this, BLA succeeds wonderfully as we get to watch US Marines, along with the USAF and Army, attempt to battle back and stop the alien invasion over the course of approximately 48 hours. The aliens are clearly evil - we get to watch a bunch of television news clips where they can be seen gunning down civies - so this is definitely not one of your pansy, politically correct Avatar-like flicks where the humans are the bad guys - and I am tremendously grateful for that moral clarity. These aliens are bad and we are good. Now it's time to kill 'em.    But in addition to this being a tribute of 50s-era sci-fi, this movie is also a tribute to classic war flicks. As you watch BLA, you will find yourself thinking less in terms of Independence Day and more along the lines of Battleground, Black Hawk Down, or The Hurt Locker. In other words, this movie is, first and foremost, a military sci fi flick, the likes of which we haven't seen since James Cameron's classic Aliens. This is a movie about war and the men who have to fight it battle by battle, and not about soldiers that have time for existential ponderings or deep discussions about the \\"nature of war.\\" Nor is it about giant robots or befuddled Jedi warriors. Thank the maker!    Helping the movie is the fact that it looks and sounds great. The FX are impressive, so much so that I was surprised this wasn't one of those 80 or 90 minute smash and grabs, but rather a movie that just ran short of two hours. The visuals do an excellent job of bringing the war to life with vim and vigor, in the process creating some very memorable scenes. I particularly enjoyed the realization of the aliens, not that they were particularly original - there is a lot of District 9 to them - but the fact that they were the analogues of the US Marines. That is, unlike other movies that seem to get carried away by flights of fantasy when it comes to imagining an alien invasion, this movie stuck to it's mil sci-fi roots and imagined the aliens to largely function like their earth military counter-parts. So we get to see alien foot-soldiers, alien officers (taller than the rest), alien drones, alien aircraft, even an incredibly cool and realistically clumsy alien crew-served heavy weapon!    Likewise, the sound FX are well done, bringing the battle to vivid life with all the appropriate sounds of war, as well as some alien noises (I particularly enjoyed the 'pop-pop' of the alien reaction thrusters ). If you have a surround sound system, crank of the volume!    Lastly, I found the cinematography to be handled well - this is where I vehemently disagree with the critics who were wont to call it \\"nauseating\\". While I don't usually care for the shaky camera documentary style, especially when we are merely watching two men having a sedate conversation (as this movie does ), once the action starts, it does an excellent job of bring the battles to frenetic life. Between the audio and visual FX, and the camera work, the battles in BLA will have you on the edge of your seat.    So, what's not to like?    First and foremost, the length of the movie. It runs for 116 minutes, which is too short! I wanted to see so much more! I mean, this is a world-wide invasion! I can imagine all sort of interesting battles, missions, guerrilla actions, and alien autopsies (there is an impromptu one in here, though ). Two hours is just too short! Fortunately, the powers that be have let it be known that this is the first of a series of films. I hope we can look forward to Battle: NY, Battle: Paris, Battle: Tokyo (all mentioned in the film). I also wish them luck with any and all game tie-ins. Maybe we'll even get some books and a TV show?    But there is a more serious problem with the running length being too short. This movie tries to cram much too much into its running time, resulting in a movie that proceeds at a breathless pace. It runs so fast that as soon as the movie begins you feel as if you came in late and need to catch up on what you missed. This is a shame for two reasons:    First, the breathless pace hurts character development. BLA is not Aliens in that there are no memorable characters outside of Sgt. Nantz. This is because the movie spends a bare 10 minutes or so introducing them to us, reducing them to \\"the guy getting married\\", \\"the friend of the guy getting married\\", \\"the raw recruit\\", etc. Even Sgt. Nanzt, well played by Aaron Echhart, lacks serious development, which is a shame because he seems to have aninteresting past involving a costly action in Afghanistan where he lost some of his men, an issue that will dog him throughout this film. The mere fact that after watching the whole film I am still hazy about just what exactly happened to Nantz is indicative of the lack of serious character development. The same goes for Corpsman (or, if you're President Obama, 'corpseman') Jibril Adukwu, a soldier of African decent who made for an interesting and likeable character. The movie needed some more time to develop these guys, but rather chose to rush off to war.    Secondly, I lament the films pace because it eliminates the opportunity to crank up the tension of the impending invasion. As with H. G. Wells classic story, as well as the two movie versions of his book, the best part of an alien invasion is knowing that it is coming and being unable to warn the characters in the film. Unfortunately, instead of the drip-drip-drip of slowly building tension and apprehension, BLA pretty much just does the equivalent of \\"a weird meteor storm is coming....this means WAR!!!!\\" >Sigh< So much wasted tension potential.    Another complaint is that is does borrow somewhat obviously from other films, particularly Aliens and District 9. For example, Michelle Rodriguez pretty much reprises the role of Pvt. Vasquez. What is more, there is a scene where the Marines take a LAV and start running over the aliens, including shooting one in the face during the process...sound familiar? And there is a District 9-esque buried spacecraft as well. Add in a demolished US airbase as in ID4, and you can pretty much conclude that when BLA borrows material, it does so unabashedly. Fortunately, most of this borrowing is in the last half hour, so it doesn't get too annoying. I guess they just started to run out of original material.    Lastly, the ending battle is a bit too contrived and rushed. I would expect an alien command center to be a tough nut to crack, but not here. It is painfully obvious thatthe film needed a feel good moment to end the story but was running out of time (or money), so they threw this together. The battle does have some good action to it, but it is ultimately yet another take on needing to shoot a photon torpedo into an exhaust shaft that is only three meters wide. Oh well. If Independence Day can be praised for its \\"sci-fi tributes\\", so can this film.    Now we come to the important question:    Why did the critics so hate this film?    I can only offer two possibilities, with the truth no doubt being somewhere in the middle:    First, this is a very patriotic movie. Here, the military men and women are portrayed as larger than life heroes, risking all to save their country. In fact, they are so heroic that, like Space Marines, they begin inspiring civilians to bravery in the face of the enemy. I have no doubt this ticked off more than a few of the more, er, cosmopolitan critics.    The second reason I think the critics disliked this movie is because of it's authentic mil sci-fi trappings. This is a film that has NOTHING in common with the juvenile sci-fi nonsense we've been force fed for years now. There aren't any teens running around with an \\"all spark\\" to save the world where the adults have failed (indeed, here we have the opposite where the older Sgt. Nantz keeps the kids in the fight and not vice versa), nor are they any lighthearted comedic moments to remind us that alien invasions can be fun, too. This is a largely grim movie (but could be grimmer - darn you PG-13!) about war. Quite frankly, I think most critics, lulled to sleep by years of fluffy Transformer and Star Wars/Star Trek flicks, just didn't understand what was going on. After all, the last real mil sci-fi we had was Cameron's Aliens...some fifteen years ago! That says a lot right there. I truly believe this film just confused the lot of them with its lack of after school content.    So there you have it, my review of Battle: Los Angeles. I liked this movie...reallyliked it. I liked it so much, that I encourage you to spend money on it via DVDs, BluRays, and VoD. The cast and crew of this movie deserve to be rewarded for making such an enjoyable film. But more than that, serious sci-fi fans need to step up and declare that if a studio has the courage to return to mature, old school sci-fi, they will be rewarded with cold, hard cash. By no means a perfect film, Battle: Los Angeles is nonetheless one worth supporting not so much for what it is, as for what it isn't.

0156347113|5.0|The \\"General Theory\\" is two things.  It's brilliant and it's complicated.  While it lays the foundation for modern economics, it's by no means a book that someone should read before they've been fairly well-versed in the subject.  While it is undoubtedly groundbreaking, that very fact makes it a book that shouldn't be read as an introduction.  The GT was for economics what relativity was for physics-- because of that, Keynes spends much of GT grappling with the orthodoxy that he later undermines.  This leads the work to be equivocating and uncertain.  Two of the major developments that come out of it are the refutation of Say's Law in Part 1.  It's impossible to overstate the importance of that development for modern macroeconomics.  It's the economics equivalent of the theory of gravity because it establishes what is essentially the modern view of recessions-- the possibility that there can be a shortfall of demand.  The second development is Keynes's formulation on financial markets, which refutes the classical assumption that financial markets are rational and perfect (in the technical sense).      The second thing to take from this review is that literally not a single one of the one or two-star reviews (aside from the ones criticizing this particular publication or the Kindle price or whatever) actually read or understood the GT.  The GT is NOT about running massive fiscal deficits or government control of the means of production.  The implication that deficits financed by government works projects in a liquidity trap (when excess demand for cash makes monetary policy ineffective) does naturally follow from GT, but there's nothing about deficits being forever and always desirable (indeed, Keynes would be horrified at that proposition).    Having said this, a clearer and perhaps more intuitive and straightforward derivation of Keynes's relevant conclusions can be found in the work of Paul Samuelson (the most influential economist of the second half of the 20th century) from the 1950's, where he develops the neo-classical synthesis and the IS-LM model that clarifies the relationship between fiscal and monetary policy.  But once Samuelson has been mastered, the GT is the place to go to understand the economics underlying Samuelson's models.  Then one can really appreciate the significance of Keynes's insight.

 is sex in his world always this one-sided?     setting all the sex and violence aside (which is damned difficult) he never gets ANYWHERE with the plot.  everyone is running in circles, wandering, in transit, in constant upheaval.  war settles nothing.  political marriage settles nothing.  death settles nothing. stuff that starts in book one does not get resolved by book four.  it just keeps going and going, transforming into deeper mires of nothing changing.  situations might change location but they never get resolved.  IS IT THAT HARD TO RESOLVE THINGS??  just when you think he resolves one plot, he brings in the whole resurrecting the dead stuff.  another complaint is that the same types of plots play out with several different characters, to the point that he's repeating himself, even with the sibling sexual tension stuff going on with two sets of siblings.      one major source of annoyance is that one of the main characters is a cross-gender mary sue that is all-powerful and perfect and beautiful, and everyone loves her, and all men want to sleep with her (even though she's all of 14). she just sorta walks through the books with the world bowing down at her feet, and doing wild and weird things that honestly become laughable at a certain point.  you know the sort??  \\"i'm the pretty elf princess, and i'm perfect and everyone loves me, and i have pet unicorns and dragons, and my eyes are purple, and i have long silky hair\\".  it's like a role play character so stacked with stats that they can never be hurt and they are practically a god. i got bored with her really fast, and started just flipping through her chapters, scanning without reading, because she was so unrealistic it was distracting and annoying.        what did i take away from these books?  this author hates women and wants to hurt them.  he has a boob fetish, maybe even a collection.  in  his world, grown adult women are evil and manipulative, and they are not to be trusted.  they are to be used sexually and killed if at all possible.  young girls that you can control are all any man should want.      storywise, what did i take away?  good people will perish because they are trying to do the right thing.  bad people will profit and advance because the old gods love bad people.  if bad people start to try and do good, they will be severely punished.  the only way to save the world from the advancing zombies and wights and evil people from the north appears to be through throwing off the old gods and accepting the one god, the god of light that is on the rise (yeah, by the way, i suspect this story is headed for jesus land - veneer of christianity not very cleverly hidden).  the message i got was: do evil and you will live.  do good and you'll die.  the one god is the way to go (even though his biggest proponent is an evil evil evil woman who dresses in red and is beautiful and makes every man around her want to sleep with her).      i honestly wanted to like this series, and the first book was okay because he didn't start getting into grossville right away.  i kept persevering because i wanted resolution.  if an author gets more than four thousand five hundred pages into a story and hasn't resolved stuff that started in book one, page one, then it's time to move on.  i held out hoping the characters i got attached to would get some resolution, or that evil people would finally get comeuppance, and nothing like this remotely happened.      i will not read any more of this author's books, not just because of the abhorent violence and inappropriate sexual content, but because NOTHING GETS RESOLVED, EVER.

It's about kids growing up in a rural town, trying to do something special with themselves.<br /><br />My Impressions:<br /><br />The film starts out with a funeral. A child loses his mother early on. A man comes over and is immediately thrown out of the house! Right here you wonder what the heck is going on!<br /><br />We find out soon enough. Spielberg and Abrams were both into making films as kids and it stuck.<br /><br />The actual filmmaking was fun to see: they set up with funky scripts, a zombie who just would not stay dead, and a kid who wanted \\"production value\\" in all his shots! Reading the negative reviews of TESS confirmed the opinion that many Amazon reviewers react more emotionally than rationally. TESS is totally UNLIKE any other Polanski production.<br /><br />It's more BBC's Masterpiece Theatre than ROSEMARY'S BABY.<br /><br />Nastassia Kinski is, of course, physically beautiful, but it is her \\"inner self\\" that captivates in the film. Her Tess is so completely understated--and this is where other reviewers may have a problem with this production--as to be ephemeral. Despite her beauty there is an illusive quality to the characterization. She's more spirit than flesh & blood. It's as if Tess advances through life with the forgone (perhaps, subconcious) conclusion of being ultimately doomed. This is so close to Thomas Hardy's vision of fate, that I'm surprised most negative reviewers either missed it or just didn't want to see because they had a pre-existing bias against Polanski. This isn't the place to go into RP's legal woes; suffice it to say that he was more set-up than predatory.<br /><br />I thought the script still needed some work in showing motivation. I agree with reviwers who felt details of Tess' relationship are too truncated. For example, the whole \\"baby thing\\" happens so fast, it's almost like Tess never had a baby. However, this may be part of Polanski's intent on not wanting the audience to wallow in wet, 3 hanky emotion--a feature that overall I appreciated. He didn't want to create a melodrama bloated with nudity & sex scenes.<br /><br />In ways--some obvious, some not so obvious--TESS reminded me of ELIVRA MADIGAN, another impressionistic style film about a compellingly strange & doomed heroine.<br /><br />Here's a little heretical hint: The TESS I screened was a 1 Disk Director's Cut version available from Netflix--and, despite being passed around like a village hussy, it was in a spotlessly pristine condition with outstanding cinematography & exhibited none of the problems some reviewers complained about. I think there is such a divide in ratings because either your imagination gets jumpstarted and you love it, or it doesn't and its a waste of 1.5 hours of your life that you'll never get back. Sure.  I can name 50 mistakes the main characters made.  I would have acted completely different.  But a movie showing how I successfully avoided danger would make a great instructional video, but an absolutely abysmal horror movie. But could Christine really have received frequent phone calls from her doctor without Ben finding out?  Would she have had time to do as much reading and writing in her journal as she is supposed to have done? With a severed artery?  And no one notices at all that the killer has sustained a major wound.<br /><br />And so it goes in this stupid, stupid book.  Two investigators search a cabin for no particular reason, and you know they're going to make a startling discovery since they have no reason for the search.  A character attacks someone in a particularly gruesome manner, and then this strange attack somehow becomes insignificant.  And the killer ..I mean, if you just analyze which character is going to give the author the trashiest possible bang for his buck, its perfectly obvious who the killer is.  The all-too-obvious killer behaves in a manner that is inconsistent with the killer's past behavior (unlike a real-life serial killer), and, again, it's purely in the interest of trashy bang for the buck.<br /><br />I loathe books like this.  The characters are cliche. There is no emotional development whatsoever.  The only thing the book has to offer is paint-by-the-numbers reader manipulation.  I had the impression that Nesbo isn't particularly interested in his characters himself; his only interest is in creating dramatic scenes, no matter how empty-headed and improbable.  Reading something like this gives me the kind of sick feeling I would get from eating an entire box of Twinkies.  I only wish it had been obvious from the beginning that this book is nothing but junk food.

B004WJRDN2|4.0|\\"McCartney\\" was a proclamation; I can do this on my own without The Beatles and I'm starting all over again. The problem at the time is that critics missed this and expected \\"Abbey Road\\" Part II from the man who masterminded the suite at the end of side two of the album with John Lennon and George Martin.<br /><br />This deluxe edition allows us to re-evalute McCartney's first \\"offical\\" solo album (he had previously composed the soundtrack for the film \\"The Family Way\\" which was orchestrated by George Martin). It also allows us to re-evaluate it without the history of The Beatles or expectations that come with it. Let's be blunt--\\"McCartney\\" is not a brilliant album nor is it flawless but having said that it has considerable charm among its ramshackle mixture of experimental music, instrumentals and songs. The best songs here \\"Maybe I'm Amazed\\", \\"Every Night\\", \\"Oo You\\", \\"Junk\\", \\"Man We Was Lonely\\" hold their own with Paul's best material and, in a sense, are much like Lennon's album \\"Plastic Ono Band\\" (although that album was recorded and released AFTER \\"McCartney\\" in that, if you read between the lines, you can get some insight into the distress that Paul faced at the time; the pressures of The Beatles, the management issues, his continuing conflict with his former bandmates and the redemption of Linda's love which pulled him from an alcholic haze that threatened to engulf and consume him.<br /><br />The remastering of the first disc sounds extremely good--it's a marked improvement over the 1993 remasters and although it isn't quite as subtle as the DCC limited gold edition that Steve Hoffman mastered, it does take advantage of the latest analog to digital converters resulting in a nice sounding remaster that has had some of the clumsy edits and other minor flaws removed. The release done by the same Abbey Road team that did nice work on The Beatles, John Lennon and Apple remasters occasionally has some peak limiting applied to increase the volume on the softer parts of the album but avoids pushing the sound into digital distortion.<br /><br />The second disc features outtakes and some off-the-cuff recordings Paul made at the time as well as live tracks. There isn't much that was left over from the recording sessions for \\"McCartney\\" so this deluxe edition is probably the least enticing in terms of extras but what we do get is quite nice. The second disc features a goofy song Paul created \\"Women Kind\\", an early demo of \\"Suicide\\" (eventually offered to Frank Sinatra who turned it down--this is NOT the Sinatra demo however but the one that we hear a snippet of on the \\"McCartney\\" album), live versions of \\"Maybe I'm Amazed\\" from the \\"One Hand Clapping\\" documentary with Wings, \\"Hot as Sun\\" performed live with Wings as well as \\"Don't Cry Baby\\" which eventually became \\"Oo You\\".<br /><br />The DVD features Paul appearing on \\"MTV Unplugged\\" in 1990 when he was promoting \\"Flowers in the Dirt\\", footage of Wings performing in concert in 1979 and a featurette of sorts on the creation of the album. There is, of course, the deluxe booklet filled with personal and professional photos of Paul taken by Linda as well and footage of Paul, Linda their kids playing at the beach around the recording time for the album (and done at the same time that the cover photo was taken for \\"McCartney\\").<br /><br />This is a nice set and although it lacks the extensive extras that will be forthcoming from other sets (and even what was included for \\"McCartney II\\"), it's a nice set for fans avialable in a limited numbered edition.<br /><br />As Paul stepped out from behind the Beatle image with his first solo album allowing him to begin again as an artist it also put him in the crosshairs of critics who expected Paul to create Beatles music when all he was interested in was creating music.but avoids pushing the sound into digital distortion. 0812973992|5.0|Colum McCann's \\"Let the Great World Spin\\" just won the prestigious and lucrative IMPAC Prize, after already winning the National Book Award, and it's hard to imagine a more deserving book. \\"Let the Great World Spin\\" is a novel set at the time of Philippe Petit's real-life tightrope walk between the twin towers of the World Trade Center in August 1974, with the walk itself as its centerpiece. In structure, it is a series of interconnected short stories, with a recurring cast of wildly disparate characters who nevertheless share connections and destinies, sometimes in ways they cannot even guess. Its structure is similar to that of \\"Trainspotting,\\" \\"Three Junes,\\" or \\"The Imperfectionists;\\" if you disliked those books, you probably won't like \\"Let the Great World Spin.\\" But if you did like them, \\"Let the Great World Spin\\" is the best of them all, combining an elegant and varied prose style, superb dialogue, and characters of almost Dickensian vividness.<br /><br />Throughout the novel lies McCann's unspoken but aching question: are our lives controlled by blind fate, by divine providence, or by a combination of forces? McCann never tips his hand as to his own beliefs, and his characters range from the deep, mystical faith of Corrigan, a young Irish monk who ministers to the hookers and low-lifes of the Bronx, to the secular outlook of Gloria, who mourns the son she lost in Vietnam. But McCann leaves readers with an overwhelming sense of our shared humanity, and with the simple, pristine goodness of showing love and concern for each other. He is aided in this goal by his remarkable ability to enter the minds of his protagonists, such as his description of Petit's thoughts as he begins his walk: \\"The core reason for it all was beauty. Walking was a divine delight. Everything was rewritten when he was up in the air. New things were possible with the human form. It went beyond equilibrium. He felt for a moment uncreated. Another kind of awake.\\"

034552554X|3.0|There is quite a lot to like in this book, which starts out with great energy and imagination but falters just over halfway through. The use of flowers as a means of communication works well to bring the reader into Victoria's tale. And while the heroine's background provides a solid basis for sympathy, unfortunately the story moves into a mystical/fantastic realm (in terms of the effects of Victoria's floral creations on her customers) that serves to reduce both the impact and the seriousness of the primary subject matter--which is, in no small regard, both the long-term often negative effect of the foster care system and the terrible loss of trust that is a major factor in the life of so many children lacking parents. Particularly well-done is the section dealing with Victoria's introduction to motherhood, especially the nursing section. Author Diffenbaugh really nails the frantic desperation bordering on madness of a new mother of tender years. For me this is the truest section of the book. Bits and pieces before and after have moments of authenticity but as a whole the narrative doesn't entirely ring true. Because the author sets herself such a stringent standard in creating her heroine as a foundling (with the many huge emotional issues that come along with that scenario), it's hard to buy so many secondary characters endowed with fairly superhuman powers of understanding and forgiveness who tolerate Victoria's behavior. I will be interested to see what the author does next. But sadly, for me, this book has too many fairy tale aspects to be taken as seriously as the subject matter dictates.

The author describes various tools, from blogs to twitter to wikipedia, google, and search engine optimization to name but a few, that warrant study by anyone interested in incorporating social media into their personal or professional lives. The book is read easily, and by avoiding jargon or complex sentence structure is easily understandable. It does not need to be read cover to cover, and can be perused for material, including quick glances at key points sections placed at the end of each chapter. Among other things, the author adresses themes such as how Social Media has become the new \\"Inbox\\", and how Social Media increases efficiency by eliminating multiple individual redundancies. While he points out the benefits of Social Media in business, I wondered about applications in my own profession, and immediately began thinking about how Social Media will continue to transform the way medical education is provided, furthering movements in this field towards democratization of knowledge and patient empowerment. In his summary, Qualman writes that the winners in the new Socialnomic world are customers and those companies that create the best products, but the reality, in my modest opinion, is that Social Media also provides an opportunity for empowerment through education and communication for all members of society, including the underserved, in a potentially very cost-effective way. The story features two very lonely children who form a special bond over the span of about two weeks; it actually seems like love at first sight, or as close to it has the children are capable. The whole plot revolves around the protagonist Abby - a vampire - and her willingness to allow another to see her as she is. It takes time, but eventually Owen, her meek neighbor, finds himself friends with a blood-sucker.<br /><br />The movie is slow. I think it serves to add a bit more tension and creepiness. Of course, this may set off some viewers. There is very little CGI and the special effects are used to show the story, not to shine a light on the gore The acting is beautiful and haunting, and you can quickly see how the film may end. The cinematography is breathtaking, with the best use of dull color light and empty space to push the loneliness that much further. Predictability aside, I come away from watching the film seeing how two very different people can find trust and love, despite their difference.<br /><br />Do take note of the R rating. The movie puts the spotlight on the 12 year old actors, but this is not a kid's film. For every bit of violence that occurs off-camera there is a bloody scene to follow it up. The film also deals with Owen being abused horribly by fellow students.<br /><br />By far, this is my favorite vampire movie since the Lugosi original.

Day 2 has much of the magic as Day 1, if not much more so, but it leaves the reader with nothing more than a chance to grasp at disappointment. The writing in Day 2 is terrific. Instead of smooth and well-connected transitions between wonderfully written scenes and fantastic events, the story meanders up and down and around with no direction or focus. Just one fascinating but unfulfilling moment after the next. That in and of itself is an art form, but it did NOT work for me. And it's incredibly frustrating because Rothfuss is a gifted writer beyond compare. For that, Rothfuss should be applauded. The artistry stands out in a huge way, but I cannot help but believe he got bored. If you want a gratifying read, this is not the book. It will leave you wanting. At times, the story will carry on with such wonderful detail and scope, and such a great story line, that one is completely enthralled.  Then the story will just tail off in some awkward fashion leading to another chapter of events, with fuzzy dialogue and a complete lack of synergy.  Often, I was ready to put the book down and not return. But I didn't. Like Day 1, I could not put down Day 2 but for different reasons.  I was waiting for Day 2 to tie a bunch of loose ends from Day 1. That never happened. Instead, Rothfuss simply created more loose ends and decided (yes, no doubt this was intentional) to leave the reader quite disappointed. Frankly, I found it frustrating and annoying. So much so, that I may not buy Day 3 if there is one unless and until I read reviews that tell me Day 3 answers the questions in Day 1 and Day 2, and the reviews are stellar. Frankly, I doubt that will be the case.

044654146X|5.0|At the outset it must be said that the title of the book is a bit misleading. This book is not about Israel's economy, nor is it really about Israel's high technology industry. In my opinion, this book is more about the Israeli mentality and culture.     In this book, Dan Senor and Saul Singer explore the reasons behind the Israeli creativity, which has manifested itself in various achievements in various fields. Without getting into too much detail, Dan Senor and Saul Singer boiled down the reasons for Israel's creativity to a few factors:    1. \\"Chutzpah\\" (audacity)- the lack of respect for authority and the \\"Chutzpah\\" to make your voice heard when you think you're right. In other words, the attitude of \\"I know how to it better than anyone else even if I just graduated and my boss has a Ph.D. and he's 20 years older than I am\\", or the fact that a young solider has no problem to tell his commander that he's doing it all wrong.    2. The Army- almost every Israeli, when s/he reaches the age of eighteen, joins the army. Dan Senor and Saul Singer argue that the amount of responsibility that these young people face is incredible and it helps these young people face the real world once they get released from the army. Another quality that the authors contribute to the army service is the fact that a lot of combat soldiers (but not only) develop the skill of improvisation (an important skill for every combat solider), which is also nurtured, to some degree, by the top generals.    3. Immigration- in short, the constant arrival of immigrants to Israel, who are highly motivated to succeeis always good for the economy, especially when these people are highly educated such as the Russian Jews.      4. The culture acceptance of failure, and therefore, people are not afraid of starting new businesses due to the fear of failing.      There are a few more factors, but these are the important ones. By reading this book you will get valuable insights into the Israeli culture and mentality. It will enrich your knowledge about Israel in general.  Highly recommended.

B000P0J0EW|5.0|For once, the IMDB top 250 is pretty close to the truth! I am perfectly dismayed to see in Top 10 such perfectly mediocre movies, to say the least, like ''Pulp Fiction'' or ''The Good, the Bad and the Ugly'', but ''The Shawshank Redemption'' and ''The Godfather (Part 1)'' sharing the first place is one of the exceptions when popular taste seems to go hand in hand with great and profound art.    ''The Shawshank Redemption'', indeed, is one of those movies which are extremely close to perfection; perhaps the indifferent soundtrack is the only weak point but this is of no consequence. The movie has a terrific screenplay, to begin with. Hardly is there a scene that is less than memorable, and most are unforgettable, and the dialogue is superb throughout, combining light humour with unobtrusive but far from negligible philosophical depth. I was amazed to learn that the scene with Mozart's music is not in Stephen King's original, and indeed he didn't like it. I have always thought of this scene as one of the most moving in the movie, together with the harrowing lives of those who come out after 40 or 50 years of imprisonment. It really is damn true that everything in life boils down to this ultimate question: ''Get busy living, or get busy dying?'' In addition to all that, Frank Darabont's direction is masterful. I honestly envy everybody who watches this movie for a first time without knowing any details of the story; he will be able to appreciate Darabont's genius for directing which is apt to be overlooked after numerous viewings. To take but one example: the discovery of Andy's escape and the explanation of it, with the retrospect shots, the consequences and Red's narrative, is well-nigh perfectly executed scene.    Having mentioned Andy and Red, need I add that the cast is outstanding? Too bad that Tim Robbins never attained a more prominent place in the Hollywood hall of fame, for he is a fine actor of considerable talent, but Morgan Freeman did. And it is he who delivers many of the most haunting lines here, such as his great (and so true!) speech about the definition of ''institutionalized'' culminating in ''They send you here for life, and that's exactly what they take. The part that counts, anyway.'' Few actors have the ability of Morgan Freeman to make the best of their lines without in the least exaggerating. The supporting cast is equally perfect. Bob Gunton as the warden and Clancy Brown as the chief guard, both of them profoundly sadistic and with rather perverse ideas of gratitude (though I confess I enjoyed Hadley's ''thanks''), are fantastic and so are some of the inmates such as William Sadler as the greatest fan of Hank Williams, Mark Rolston as one of the Sisters who are not homosexual because ''one must be human first and they don't qualify'' (Red) and especially James Whitmore as the old Brooks, probably the most heart-rending character in the movie.       There really is no need of wasting words. ''The Shawshank Redemption'' remains one of the most powerful tributes to human spirit ever made for the screen. Everybody MUST see this gem at least once. It is an incredibly inspiring movie, a movie that gives me hope (yes, the best of things!) in human nature like few other works of art, let alone personal experiences, do. Like another equally shattering masterpiece (''One Flew over Cuckoo's Nest''), it reminds us, the free men who rather presumptuously calls ourselves ''normal'', that prisons and lunatic asylums are full, not so much with monsters of depravity, but mostly with ordinary people like you and me who just did one stupid thing more or for whom the pressure of society turned out to be too much; to say nothing of the ridiculously inept prison system rife with callousness and cruelty that range from terror behind the bars to starving outside of them. What better way to finish than one memorable quote from Red's ''institutionalized speech'':    ''These walls are funny. First you hate 'em, then you get usedto 'em. Enough time passes, you get so you depend on them. That's institutionalized.''

0312360258|3.0|Imagine a world where monsters are an accepted reality. Imagine, too, that in this world, children undergo an alarming change. No longer are they the sweet-tempered cherubs you have come to treasure, but dark, brooding creatures that wreak havoc in the lives of others and have the habit of sleeping during the day. What are they? No, not teenagers (though you're not far from the mark if that was your guess): vampires. Welcome to the world of Zoey Redbird, a place not so different from our own: bullies pick on nerds, parents and children fail to understand each other, and the high school \\"in\\" crowd takes particular joy in excluding outsiders from social activities. The only notable difference is that the word \\"vampires\\" is spelled with a \\"y,\\" and teenagers have the nasty habit of turning into these bloodsucking creatures without warning. This \\"Change,\\" occurring only during adolescence, is caused by a confusing mix of mutant DNA and hormones.  When Zoey comes down with a particularly bad case of it, her horrified family and friends turn on her. She takes refuge at the House of Night, an exclusive boarding school for vampyres, where she makes a friends, enemies, and a name for herself. Unfortunately, her story isn't a very remarkable one. It borrows elements from a number of successful fantasy sagas, and more or less reads like an American Harry Potter: lonely, friendless protagonist discovers that s/he has magical powers and gets to attend a school for magic where students wear elaborately designed school uniforms. To the protagonist's surprise, s/he turns out to have an outstanding talent for magic, despite the fact that s/he has had no formal training. In addition, the protagonist acquires, upon immediate arrival, an arrogant, blond-haired nemesis with inappropriate levels of hostility towards non-magic users. To top this all off, Zoey has a \\"mark\\" on her forehead, a crescent moon-shaped tattoo indicative of the Change. So: is this just another exercise in teenage wish-fulfillment? You bet. But like the countless other offerings in the YA vampire romance genre, it serves as a sufficient mode of escapism. Why else should we read, if not to escape reality? However, a word of warning: although this novel will doubtless appeal to the legions of readers suffering from post-bestselling-fantasy-saga syndrome, this series is more appropriate for the Ages 16-18 bracket. Aside from its frequent use of strong language and sexual references (we first meet Zoey's chief rival, Aphrodite, in a public hallway trying to force her ex-boyfriend into accepting a sexual favor), the novel's themes will be better appreciated by those about to graduate from high school. The change of scene from the human world to the House of Night mirrors the what an average teen experiences during the transition from high school, where loneliness and uncertainties abound, to college, where most outcasts finally find their \\"niche.\\" Marked, the first book in the House of Night series, will appeal to any teen who has ever found themselves bored, friendless, or lonely, and transports them from this mundane world of ours into a land of night where good friends abound, rivals are two-dimensional and unworthy of your presence, and hot vampyre guys have eyes only for you.ish-fulfillment? For this review my focus is only going to deal with the issue of slavery and how it has been sanitized and trivialized in this movie.     Set in 1806 England, and although, one never actually see's any slaves, or a black person at all for that matter; they are the very foundation of the Bertram family's wealth. A family that is rich enough so that they can afford to take in a very poor 10 year old relation. Which brings me to the start of the movie and the first mention of slaves, as the coach is carrying the 10 year old girl Fanny Price to her rich relative's mansion.  They pass along a coast where a ship is anchored and the coachman stops to tend to the horses and Fanny leans out the window and asks, \\"Do you hear that? It was the sound of wailing, the coachman explains to her that it was coming from the anchored ship in which slaves were confined. My immediate thought was, is this the new way the subject of slavery is going to be dealt with in films now?     Then further along in this story, we come to find out how much the business of slaveholding and dealing in Antigua has aided and given this family respectability and standing in English society. The daughters and sons have all been given the best education; they are full of all the right social graces and good and witty converse. They have been introduced to polite society at the right moment, and have every expectation to aspire to the best financial matches for their future matrimonies, which will increase their fortunes, all thanks to the poor slaves being held in Antigua.      The next mention of the slaves in Antigua is when the Patriarch has to travel to Antigua to go over his holdings. Upon his return from Antigua there is a scene where the family is in the sitting room, and the Patriarch is talking about the physical attributes of the slaves he holds and how there is a girl he plans on bringing to England to work in the house, and Fanny Price mentions that she has read that if you bring a slave to England they would have to be granted freedom. Then, the Patriarch makes an ignorant comment about how two mulattoes could not have offspring, to which statement one of his son's who is studying to become a clergyman objects.     The fourth mention I noted is when Fanny Price who was accustomed to reading books in the great library of the mansion finds a book of sketches of slaves, and the daily tribulations they suffer, she is so overcome that she begins to cry as she turns the pages and finds one sketch after another more gruesome than the last. The patriarch walks up on her, and is angered that she has uncovered his book of \\"slaveography\\", after rudely sending her out of the library he is seen to be overcome with feelings of shame and starts to tear each page from the sketch book and throws it into the lit fireplace. Then he is seen to be in a state of remorse and guilt as the camera pans away from him sitting alone in the library.     The last scene to trivialize the unfortunate slaves in Antigua was towards the end, when Fanny Price is neatly putting in place the end of each character in the film for the sake of the viewer. She is telling how each one ended up, when she casually mentions that the Patriarch has now divested himself of the business of slave holding and trading and had found a new lucrative business concern which was tobacco, another slave related business!    \\"Djonga (Slavery)\\", a song by Salifu Keita which shows up at the end as the credits are running is one of the brighter spots of this film.

0765350386|4.0|I've been hearing about Brandon Sanderson's fantasy efforts for several years now and didn't really decide to give him a go until news broke of his having been recruited to complete Robert Jordan's Wheel of Time saga.<br /><br />After researching his first two stand-alone pieces (Elantris and Warbreaker) I became disinterested in his whole brand of fantasy on account of the lack of genre purity present: Sanderson seemed fond of well constructed magical systems but seemed to cross into a bit of science fiction/ zombie horror/ humor in his prose.  Call it clich&eacute; if you must, but I typically aim for fantasy that is formulaic, epic in length, dry and fairly boring (hence my appreciation for Wheel of Time).<br /><br />My plan of attack was to wait for him to complete his first series, which turned out to be the Mistborn trilogy.  Once TOR saw fit to release the entire collection in a single paperback boxset, I decided I could ignore the recommendations from friends no longer.  Ready or not, I was going to jump into the realms of Brandon Sanderson.<br /><br />I will start with my initial feelings and work up to what made the book so enjoyable for me (and what makes it come so highly recommended).  Sanderson writes with a very flatfoot style for better or worse.  The good news is it is very easy to read, almost conversational by nature.  Sanderson narrates his tales as if he were recounting events with some pals during a camping trip opposed to the egotistical, borderline omnipotent tones you find in most epic fantasies.<br /><br />I personally appreciate this approach, as there is no old English nonsense and certainly no sentences structured simply to showcase the author's vocabulary (or impressive thesaurus skills).  The downside is don't expect poetry here either.  Word choices were clearly made to deliver a point, not dazzle the reader with descriptive prowess.<br /><br />Pacing is odd at best with the first half of the book struggling to find its rhythm.  I won't lie: I very nearly gave up at around the half-way point due to Sanderson's penchant for repeating the same points over and over and devoting entire 20+ page chapters to character planning and scheming.  Looking back now, it was probably done in attempt to fill pages and to stretch out the story a bit considering once things start happening, they happen quickly.  By the end of the book, I was hard pressed to put it down in fact.<br /><br />Unlike say George RR Martin or Steven Erikson, Sanderson doesn't feel the need to flop his tale across dozens of character viewpoints either.  In fact Mistborn is essentially told from a single perspective; that of teenage female Vin and her transformation from simple slave-girl to powerful sorceress.<br /><br />He manages to build a pretty interesting political & social structure along the way without relying on the massive scope associated with following multiple factions or warring legions and battalions.<br /><br />As surely summaries and countless critiques have suggested, magic plays an integral part of the prose and true to form, Sanderson delivers once again in the arena of a magic system that borders on science fiction.  This one in particular, Allomancy as it is called, is basically a melding of metallurgy with alchemy and a bit of digestive system workings added for good measure.  In and of itself, it's a solid idea but where Sanderson shines is in his ability to effectively describe fairly complicated concepts such as how to control bursts of flight or see a few moments into the future.<br /><br />The factor that I feel most lukewarm about would have to be the reliance upon the \\"heist\\" element; which bears a remarkable resemblance to \\"The Lies of Locke Lamora\\" by Scott Lynch.  This was probably coincidental as the novels were released within a few weeks of one another back in 2007, but it adds a slight young adult/ juvenility to an otherwise very adult conflict.<br /><br />Early on I feared that Sanderson was merelyanother example in the latest crop of modern fantasy writers (Patrick Rothfuss, Joe Abercrombie, and said Scott Lynch to mention a few) who water down their fantasy elements with thinly masked pop culture references and humor attempts but fortunately Sanderson rises above these pitfalls in his own way.<br /><br />There are some truths to complaints that Sanderson really shortchanges the secondary characters' depth and development but overall, after having just completed the tale last night, it is the main cast that carries the core through and through.<br /><br />Perhaps the greatest attribute is Sanderson's ability to create a genuine sense of hopelessness.  A lowly child overthrowing an entire empire is nothing new in fantasy but here we are given a truly hopeless cause that becomes even more hopeless as the story progresses.  The last Â¾ of the book sets up a situation that will have even the most veteran of fantasy aficionados wondering how in the world Sanderson's going to pull it all together.  Take comfort that not only does he succeed but does so in expert style.<br /><br />Readers who fear that because this is a series rather than a stand-alone piece, it is destined to end on a cliffhanger need not be concerned.  Sanderson makes certain to tie up any potential loose ends by this book's conclusion.<br /><br />In all, a highly recommended read for fantasy devotes and non-fans alike for its ability to pull off quite an impressive conclusion.  Keep in mind that it doesn't start off with the type of momentum one would expect from a novel with this type of hype surrounding it, but it culminates into a very impressive piece of modern fantasy.Abercrombie, and said Scott Lynch to mention a few) who water down their fantasy elements with thinly masked pop culture references and humor attempts but fortunately Sanderson rises above these pitfalls in his own way. The last Â¾ of the book sets up a situation that will have even the most veteran of fantasy aficionados wondering how in the world Sanderson's going to pull it all together. Keep in mind that it doesn't start off with the type of momentum one would expect from a novel with this type of hype surrounding it, but it culminates into a very impressive piece of modern fantasy.

6305313687|4.0|I remember when I was a kid the most I knew of American History X was the curb stomping scene. I didn't know anything more about the movie, so prior to seeing the movie the message was obviously not being translated properly within the minds of my peers. Looking back, though, that was fine by me as I'm mulatto and the friends who introduced the stories of American History X to me were all white, meaning they didn't have any of the personal issues the film mainly referred to.    Recently though, I mean within a week of writing this review I have a friend who told me that he would have loved this movie had it not been for the ending (he didn't quite say it that way, but the ending ruined it for him which is the same in my small little mind). I think this is largely due to the editing of the movie putting the focus in an area that kind of throws our reasoning of the movie slightly off.  For those of you who may not have seen American History X here's a little break down of the plot: Derek Vinyard (Edward Norton), an open white supremacist, has been in prison for several years due to the voluntary manslaughter of a couple of black people who tried to carjack him. On the day of his release his brother Danny (Edward Furlong), after turning in an assignment advocating Hitler's Mein Kampf, is required to write a history paper based on Derek's experience.    First, I would like to say that this film is brilliant. In its exploration of white supremacy it views all of our stereotyping as a gateway to more virulent hate. Despite it's, at times, extremely violent imagery, X is very much a call for reform in the way that we think of race and of each other as individuals. Many people will, and have, found this movie preachy, but in my opinion it's not as preachy as other race related movies that others have \\"campaigned\\" against that I have enjoyed (i.e. Crash).    The ending is still quite a point of contention though, and with good reason. As I mentioned before, I think this owesa lot to the editing of this movie. Unfortunately, director Tony Kaye was unable to offer an edit of the movie that the studio was willing to work with, and other editors were brought in and Norton assisted with the editing. The result was an edit of the movie that was extremely Norton heavy, although the finished movie was still thoroughly enjoyable. That also meant the message of the movie was fairly diluted.    Viewing the movie from my perspective, though, the story was actually speaking to everyone trying to reform Danny through Derek's story. Sure partially to the movie's detriment the story is overly focused on Derek, but Danny is our way into the story of the movie and he also represents the audience. He's exploring the story of his brother, as we are, because he needs to learn something from it. The whole reason for that exploration: to turn back before it's too late.    It's easy to miss that point in the wake of the ending, but if you allow yourself to bypass the end, you'd realize that it really represents the seemingly never ending circulation of hate. Trying not to give away the ending, this is really what we see. Danny has given in to the story of Derek and has freed himself of the hate that once consumed him, but he passed it on to another person who willingly partakes of that hate. It's a cycle that we need to break, and that's really what the ending, including the ending Abraham Lincoln quote signifies.    I highly recommend this movie. I know some people will find it contrived, and a little too on the nose, but I feel there is quite a bit of drama to offset the preachiness of this film. The only other issue that I had with this particular film concerns a score that over emphasizes moments of the movie as grandiose or too foreboding considering the more documentary like style of the shoot. Overall, though, I think it effectively evokes emotion while conveying a message to end the cycle of hate.    4.5/5    For more review check out: <...>
